    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I have read the Discworld books in series, however I think you can read the sub series in each of their order. However I have noticed, especially late<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I have read the Discworld books in series, however I think you can read the sub series in each of their order. However I have noticed, especially later in the series, there are always references that happened in the earlier books, even outside of the sub series but they don't have impact on the story. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_101060304" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060304" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101060304?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385608671" href="/book/show/833475.Thud_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2014-reads">2014-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2011-reads">2011-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2013-reads">2013-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2019-reads">2019-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2016-reads">2016-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2017-reads">2017-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=pratchett">pratchett</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2012-reads">2012-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2015-reads">2015-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101060304" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4039501882446552156"><blockquote> <b><i>"What kind of creature defines itself by hatred?"</i></b>
</blockquote>That is the overarching theme of Terry Pratchett's <i>Thud!</i> which is indisputably one of his best works - in content and execution and the amount of serious thinking it makes me do after seeing the usual 'mundane' things through the prism of Pratchett's writing.<br/><br/>Like other Pratchett's later works, this book is a departure from the light-hearted humorous comedic tone of earlier Discworld books which initially were just smart and witty parodies of fan</span>
  <span id="freeText4039501882446552156" style="display:none"><blockquote> <b><i>"What kind of creature defines itself by hatred?"</i></b>
</blockquote>That is the overarching theme of Terry Pratchett's <i>Thud!</i> which is indisputably one of his best works - in content and execution and the amount of serious thinking it makes me do after seeing the usual 'mundane' things through the prism of Pratchett's writing.<br><br>Like other Pratchett's later works, this book is a departure from the light-hearted humorous comedic tone of earlier Discworld books which initially were just smart and witty parodies of fantasy genre conventions but have easily transcended that since. Yes, the witty remarks, dry humor and the moments of pure comedic genius are still unquestionably there, but the overall tone has changed.<b> It's more serious. It gets angry. It has sharp edges. It's not afraid to bite. It wants to make you think - and maybe chuckle once in a while during it. </b>The comedy this time has met the tragedy.<br><br>And no wonder, for the material it tackles is not light-hearted at all.<br><br><b>If I had just one word to sum it up the theme it would be Darkness.</b><br>- Of course, the darkness of underground mines and caverns - the obvious one.<br>- The horrible darkness of bigotry and hatred and prejudice and ethnic conflicts. <br>- The intolerant darkness of any sort of fundamentalism.<br>- The oppressive darkness of political intrigues that plow right over the lives of ordinary persons.<br>- The seductive darkness of mindless revenge.<br>- And, of course, the hidden darkness that lurks inside each person. Even inside Sam Vimes. Especially Sam Vimes. <blockquote>
  <i>"No excuses. No excuses at all. Once you had a good excuse, you opened the door to bad excuses."</i>
</blockquote>Commander of Ankh Morpork City Watch (and also the Duke of Ankh and former Blackboard Monitor), Sam Vimes is a straight-as-an-arrow copper who may not be the sharpest knife in the drawer (<i>so they say, but *they* may not always be right</i>) but who is <b>your go-to man when manure hits the windmill</b>. After all, <i>"When people are trying to kill you, it means you’re doing something right. It was a rule Sam had lived by."</i> He has been through a lot since his days as a cynical disillusioned drunk in the once-dysfunctional Ankh-Morpork Watch. As time went by, he gained a lot - a city that miraculously functions and manages to remain stable, loyal colleagues who became dear friends, and family that provides him the blessing he is afraid he does not deserve. <b>But all things gained can be lost, and that's something that would worry him quite a bit.</b><blockquote>
  <i>"Vimes had never got on with any game much more complex than darts. Chess in particular had always annoyed him. It was the dumb way the pawns went off and slaughtered their fellow pawns while the kings lounged about doing nothing that always got to him; if only the pawns united, maybe talked the rooks round, the whole board could've been a republic in a dozen moves."</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1396698391i/9173762._SX540_.jpg" width="330" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>And this time Sam Vimes has enough to worry about. <br><br>Ankh-Morpork is not a good place to be right now, with tensions from the centuries-old ethnic conflicts (in Discworld fashion, those are between the dwarfs and the trolls) at the all-time high as the anniversary of Koom Valley is nearing. Koom Valley was a place where centuries ago the dwarfs ambushed the trolls. Or perhaps the trolls ambushed the dwarfs. Nobody knows.<b> What they do know is that they are supposed to hate each other - because it's always been so. For them, the battle of Koom Valley never ended, and Sam Vimes would do anything to prevent it from replaying in the streets of Ankh-Morpork. </b><blockquote>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1396700720i/9174050._SX540_.jpg" width="340" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>The no-idea-who-ambushed-whom Battle of Koom Valley seemed like a throwaway joke in the earlier Discworld books. But here Pratchett takes full advantage of it, turning the funny on its head, making it serious and even tragic at times. Because Koom Valley still lives on in the minds of people despite hundreds of miles and hundreds of years, and still claims its victims as the dead bodies pile up and hatred is sparked by those who think they see the light (or rather, the darkness), and riots are about to tear the city apart and turn it into another battle of Koom Valley, and Sam Vimes is not having it, and not even ancient supernatural entities can stop him.<blockquote>
  <i>‘Given, then, a contest between an invisible and very powerful quasi-demonic thing of pure vengeance on the one hand, and the commander on the other, where would you wager, say…one dollar?’<br>‘I wouldn’t, sir. That looks like one that would go to the judges.’ </i>
</blockquote>Pratchett's Ankh-Morpork world by the time of <i>Thud!</i> has become incredibly complex and developed - to the point where the portrait of the city in my mind is as vivid as though I have really lived there. The characters are unbelievably alive, complex, multilayered, evolving, developing, growing, and very real (yes, real - despite this being a fantasy world, it's firmly grounded in sometimes whacky reality). Pratchett excels at so seamlessly combining the comedy and suspense and drama and tragedy (often within a single page) that the effect is mesmerizing and undeniably incredibly memorable. <blockquote>
  <i>“The important thing is not to shout at this point, Vimes told himself. Do not…what do they call it…go postal? Treat this as a learning exercise. Find out why the world is not as you thought it was. Assemble the facts, digest the information, consider the implications. THEN go postal. But with precision.” </i>
</blockquote><br>The little details - from the father's unerring love for his child <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(Vimes' semi-delirious rendition of 'Where's My Cow?' amidst the destruction in the caves under Koom Valley and his chilling moment of realization that the assassin would easily target his small child) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, to Detritus' war on drugs and troll graffiti <i>(“Mr. Shine! Him Diamond!”)</i>, to the desperation of a gamer dwarf who is so good playing as a troll in the chess-like game of Thud and who cannot quite reconcile what he is with what he is expected to be, to the little pedantic office clerk with the heart of a lion, to the guilt that can literally kill, to silly discussions about what constitutes art, to Vimes' careful and never ending battle to keep the darkness in despite everything around him that so easily could be fixed by just one flash of mindless rage - <b>all these details combine so beautifully into a fascinating and vivid picture that tells a story so good it's a real (un)expected treasure.</b><br><br>A treasure just like a certain painting by an unfortunate artist whose life can be summed up as such - <i>"Methodia Rascal: born, painted famous picture, thought he was a chicken, died."</i> A treasure because, if nothing else, it makes me think once more about the need for guarding the darkness like a true Blackboard Monitor Watchman.<blockquote>
  <i>'Quis custodiet ipsos custodes? Your grace.'<br>'I know that one,' said Vimes. 'Who watches the watchmen? Me, Mr. Pessimal.'<br>'Ah, but who watches you, your grace?' said the inspector, with a brief smile.<br>'I do that, too. All the time,' said Vimes. 'Believe me.'</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1396700720i/9174051.jpg" width="330" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>Terry Pratchett is an amazing writer, an excellent storyteller. The craft of writing in his hands is precisely that - not a nebulous semi-magical art (after all, it's got no urns in it, just ask Fred Colon and Nobby!) but a true well-honed masterful craft. And that in my opinion is the sheer awesome greatness. And I love it.<br><br><b>And I will continue finding reasons to quote Pratchett on daily basis. Because he's that good.</b><blockquote>
  <i>“I am the Summoning Dark.” It was not, in fact, a sound, but had it been, it would have been a hiss. “Who are you?”<br>“I am the Watchman.”<br>“They would have killed his family!” The darkness lunged, and met resistance. “Think of the deaths they have caused! Who are you to stop me?”<br>“He created me. Quis custodiet ipsos custodes? Who watches the watchmen? Me. I watch him. Always. You will not force him to murder for you.”<br>“What kind of human creates his own policeman?”<br>“One who fears the dark.”<br>“And so he should,” said the entity, with satisfaction.<br>“Indeed. But I think you misunderstand. I am not here to keep the darkness out. I am here to keep it in.” There was a clink of metal as the shadowy watchman lifted a dark lantern and opened its little door. Orange light cut through the blackness. “Call me… the Guarding Dark. Imagine how strong I must be.”<br>The Summoning Dark backed desperately into the alley, but the light followed it, burning it.<br>“And now,” said the watchman, “get out of town.”</i>
</blockquote>————————<br><u>2016 edit:</u><br><br>On my reread #8 (!) I still can't help but marvel at the heap of awesome this book is. Undoubtedly the absolute high of Pratchett's writing. This is the book that does not seem likely to lose its relevance in the world like ours, where, sadly, we see the thriving of the hatred and prejudice and insistence on fixating on what separates us from each other fueled by the waves of bigoted populism.<br><br>Maybe it will get better one day.<br>————————<br><u>2020 edit:</u><br><br>The more I reread it, the more I love it, and I didn’t think it would even be possible. What a truly brilliant book. Sir Terry, you were a genius indeed.<br><br>————<br>My ever-expanding collection of Pratchett’s Discworld reviews:<br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060202" rel="nofollow noopener">Guards! Guards!</a> <br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060297" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060304" rel="nofollow noopener">Thud!</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/157609988" rel="nofollow noopener">Lords and Ladies</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/160703976" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wee Free Men</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101060281" rel="nofollow noopener">Hogfather</a><br>- <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/312984829" rel="nofollow noopener">Monstrous Regiment</a></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="4039501882446552156" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1964635191" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1964635191" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1964635191?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1964635191" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7543938195002965152">Sam Vines is one of the greatest examples of leadership that can be found amongst any of the SF/F protagonists in the past 50 years.<br/><br/>Pratchett’s main character of his City Watch sub-series, Commander of the Watch Sam Vines, Duke of Ankh-Morpork, who arose from the captain of the night watch, who used to give part of his monthly check to the widows and orphans of fallen watchmen, is again at center stage in this the 34th Discworld novel first published in 2006 and good enough to get a nomination f</span>
  <span id="freeText7543938195002965152" style="display:none">Sam Vines is one of the greatest examples of leadership that can be found amongst any of the SF/F protagonists in the past 50 years.<br><br>Pratchett’s main character of his City Watch sub-series, Commander of the Watch Sam Vines, Duke of Ankh-Morpork, who arose from the captain of the night watch, who used to give part of his monthly check to the widows and orphans of fallen watchmen, is again at center stage in this the 34th Discworld novel first published in 2006 and good enough to get a nomination for the Locus Award in the same year.<br><br>Now happily married and wrapped around the little finger of his baby boy, little Sam, Vimes has quit drinking but is still very much the low pretense, practical man of the watch, more at home walking the streets of his city than in the Patrician’s office or gussied up for a court appearance. Pratchett has drawn Vimes as an everyman of leadership, a copper’s copper, able to knock some heads together and also fill out a overtime sheet for a shift.<br><br>In this episode of Pratchett’s wildly successful Discworld universe, Vimes finds himself trying to solve the murder of an influential and controversial Dwarf leader. The murder comes on the eve of the Koom Valley battle anniversary, the epic and legendary struggle between Dwarves and Trolls. The Dwarf and Troll communities in Ankh-Morpork are bristling for a fight, as ancient animosities seethe and boil to the surface. Vimes’s leadership is put to the test mightily but Pratchett has drawn him from tougher stuff and this is one of Pratchett's best Discworld novels.<br><br>Using the Troll-Dwarf vendetta as an allegory for racism, ethnic strife and real life feuds, Pratchett also explores themes of loyalty, leadership, and extremism. Not just for Discworld fans, this is a good book period.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1548252198i/26960056._SY540_.jpg" width="285" height="400" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="7543938195002965152" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_571288467" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/571288467" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/571288467?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2020-shelf">2020-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer571288467" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7171046528255534215">Jeeze, every time I read a Discworld novel, it's always like coming back to a very funny home full of angry dwarves and pissed trolls doing their best to get drunk and start a war over some ancient grudge that no one alive actually understands.<br/><br/>In other words, like Thanksgiving Dinner.<br/><br/>Or something like that.<br/><br/>Indeed, it's actually a police procedural with the glorious Vimes as he tries to stop another civil war on the streets of Ankh-Morpork the best way he can... by cutting through all the red tap</span>
  <span id="freeText7171046528255534215" style="display:none">Jeeze, every time I read a Discworld novel, it's always like coming back to a very funny home full of angry dwarves and pissed trolls doing their best to get drunk and start a war over some ancient grudge that no one alive actually understands.<br /><br />In other words, like Thanksgiving Dinner.<br /><br />Or something like that.<br /><br />Indeed, it's actually a police procedural with the glorious Vimes as he tries to stop another civil war on the streets of Ankh-Morpork the best way he can... by cutting through all the red tape and bull-heading his way through every single problem.<br /><br />Or going berserk while yelling a kid's bedtime story, thoroughly destroying the morale of his enemies.<br /><br />It's the little things.<br /><br />I love these books. :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="7171046528255534215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_435878553" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/435878553" />
    <a title="Ms. Smartarse" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13703358-ms-smartarse"><img alt="Ms. Smartarse" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1401273675p2/13703358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/435878553?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ms. Smartarse" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ms. Smartarse" href="/user/show/13703358-ms-smartarse">Ms. Smartarse</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=part_of_a_series">part_of_a_series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=spiritual">spiritual</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=racism">racism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13703358-ms-smartarse?shelf=politics">politics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer435878553" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17967519133499794965">Watch Commander Sam Vimes has had just about enough of Koom Valley. It has always been a contentious subject with both dwarves and trolls, but <em>these</em> days even the mere <em>thought</em> of it has become dangerous. Add in a suspicious murder, and the Commander of the City Watch is rightly expecting a full-blown reenactment of the legendary battle to take place at any moment.<br/><br/>Lord Vetinari on the other hand, seems much more concerned with the lack of vampire coppers, or the proper financial audit of the Watc</span>
  <span id="freeText17967519133499794965" style="display:none">Watch Commander Sam Vimes has had just about enough of Koom Valley. It has always been a contentious subject with both dwarves and trolls, but <em>these</em> days even the mere <em>thought</em> of it has become dangerous. Add in a suspicious murder, and the Commander of the City Watch is rightly expecting a full-blown reenactment of the legendary battle to take place at any moment.<br><br>Lord Vetinari on the other hand, seems much more concerned with the lack of vampire coppers, or the proper financial audit of the Watch. So the Patrician sees fit to 'throw' one of his most trusted and boring financial inspectors in the mix, because obviously our hero doesn't have nearly enough to worry about. <br><br>Yet amidst all this chaos, the most pressing question on the Commander's mind is: how to fit in everything before Little Sammy's 6 o'clock story time?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1517346861i/24969599.jpg" alt="Where's my Cow?!" width="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/62530.Thud___Discworld___34__City_Watch__7_" title="Thud! (Discworld, #34; City Watch #7) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Thud!</a> has been one of my least favorite Discworld novels, but reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1271759159" rel="nofollow noopener">Carol.'s review of Night Watch</a>, I figured it deserved a second chance. Sure enough, a reread of the books from the City Watch subseries, in the proper chronological order, made a significant difference:<br>- I no longer pictured Captain Carrot Ironfoundersson as a perpetually mopey guy wearing a carrot costume <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1517346861i/24969598._SX540_.png" alt="Captain Carrot 1st vs 2nd read" width="350" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>- I got all the funny references to previous books<br>- the political jokes actually hit their mark, this time around<br><br>... <em>buuut</em>, I didn't enjoy reading it nearly as much as I did <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47989.Night_Watch__Discworld___29__City_Watch___6_" title="Night Watch (Discworld, #29; City Watch, #6) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Watch</a>. As I had only retained very vague memories from the first reread, I'm fairly certain that the "lack of surprise" wasn't the reason for my lackluster enthusiasm. The lack of Carrot x Angua romantic sub-plot however <em>may</em> have been the cause. While in Night Watch, this aspect was expected, in here I would've wanted a bit more 'tangible' proof of Carrot's feelings, rather than just that one stray comment from Sally.<br><br><strong>Score: 3.4 / 5 stars</strong><br><br>It's definitely not as boring as I remembered, and for those of you who like to play the champion for the down-trodden, quite a treat. <br><br>A 2nd reread, after catching up with the previous books of the sub-series, improved things significantly, unfortunately not quite enough to deserve an additional star in rating. I had previously rated it 2.7/5 stars, back in 2012.<br><br>========================================<br>Other books featuring the Night Watch:<br>Review of the 1st book: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/848814884" rel="nofollow noopener">Guards!Guards!</a><br>Review of the 2nd book: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1135571640" rel="nofollow noopener">Men at Arms</a><br>Review of the 3rd book: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1212951873" rel="nofollow noopener">Feet of Clay</a><br>Review of the 4th book: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1313943620" rel="nofollow noopener">Jingo</a><br>Review of the 5th book: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1319112593" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fifth Elephant</a><br>Review of the 6th book: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1319112623" rel="nofollow noopener">Night Watch</a><br>Review of the 8th book: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1319112687" rel="nofollow noopener">Snuff</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17967519133499794965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1672076967" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1672076967" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1672076967?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060815318" href="/book/show/386375.Thud_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1672076967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7217544700832661865"><br/><b> The war between the dwarfs and the trolls was a battle of natural forces, like the war between the wind and the waves. It had a momentum of its own. </b> <br/><br/>It's origins are hidden in the mists surrounding the mythical Koom Valley, a place where each side is accusing the other of treacherous ambush, but its recent manifestation has moved to Ankh-Morpork, courtesy of the city's new policies of welcoming immigrants.<br/><br/><b> Pretty soon some people will be saying: Who let all these dwarfs in here? They undermine</b></span>
  <span id="freeText7217544700832661865" style="display:none"><br /><b> The war between the dwarfs and the trolls was a battle of natural forces, like the war between the wind and the waves. It had a momentum of its own. </b> <br /><br />It's origins are hidden in the mists surrounding the mythical Koom Valley, a place where each side is accusing the other of treacherous ambush, but its recent manifestation has moved to Ankh-Morpork, courtesy of the city's new policies of welcoming immigrants.<br /><br /><b> Pretty soon some people will be saying: Who let all these dwarfs in here? They undermine our city and they don't obey our laws. And the trolls? We used to chain 'em up like guard dogs, and now they're allowed to walk around threatening real people. </b><br /><br />Recent events (Dallas Police killings 2016, Brexit) make this Discworld book published in 2005 almost prescient. Ankh-Morpork is a city about to explode into racial violence after the murder of a dwarf fundamentalist leader by an alleged troll intruder. All of this taking place on the eve of the Battle of Koom Valley anniversary.<br /><br /><i> Young dwarfs listened to him, because he talked of history and destiny and all the other words that always got trotted out to put a gloss on slaughter. It was heady stuff, except that brains weren't involved. Malign idiots like him were the reason you saw dwarfs walking around now not just with the 'cultural' battle-axes but heavy mail, chains, morning stars, broadswords ... all the dumb, in-your-face swaggering that was known as 'clang'.<br />Trolls listened, too. You saw more lichen, more clan graffiti, more body carving, and much, much bigger clubs being dragged around. </i><br /><br />Malign idiots (Trump?) are pushing the street gangs of trolls and dwarfs towards open violence but Ankh-Morpork has something that we in the real world are sorely missing: a real hero, a man of principle and integrity, a man of common sense and common decency who would not stand back and allow his city to crumble into dust around him, a man even the leaders of the crime underwold in Ankh-Morpork are turning to when the going gets rough. Here is Chrysophrase the troll drug dealer in a scene reminiscent of The Godfather:<br /><br /><i> An' den I hear my ol' friend Mister Vimes is on der case and I am thinkin', dat Mister Vimes, he may be very insensitive to de nu-unces of troll culture sometimes, but der man is straight as a arrow and der are on him no flies. [...] But der name Vimes ... dat name means a lot. Can't be bribed, he once arrested the Patrician, not der sharpest knife in the drawer but honest like anything and he don't stop digging. </i><br /><br />Sam Vimes is rapidly becoming my favorite character in the whole Discworld series and I would vote for him for President in an eye-blink. Sam wouldn't want the job though. He belongs to the City Watch, has dedicated his life to Law and Order and he believes politics stinks.<br /><br />Politics, courtesy of the Lord Vetinary, is saddling Vimes with a new positive-action recruit representing a hated minority and with an internal audit of the Watch expense accounts. To complicate matters even more, Sam Vimes has to comply with Lady Sybill's request that he sits to have his portrait painted and has to learn to deal with new technology in the form of his <b> Dis-Organizer Mark Five, the Gooseberry TM. </b>. It makes for a busy daily schedule that somehow has to leave a window for the most important thing of all: Sam's six o'clock sharp meeting with his son:<br /><br /><i> Young Sam was standing up in his cot, watching the door. Vimes's day went soft and pink. </i><br /><br />Thud is at its most basic a crime story, with Sam Vimes cast in a Sam Spade type of role and the theme of ethnic war fueled by xenophobia and demagoguery make it a bone-chilling experience. Sir Terry Pratchett though manages to find the humorous side of even the darkest corners of our social conscience, and old characters mix with the new recruits to produce yet another memorable Discworld journey. <br /><br /><i> Everyone starts on the street. And on the Night Watch, too. Good training. The best there is. A week of rainy nights with the mists coming up and the water trickling down your neck and odd noises in the shadows ... well, that's when we find out if we've got a real copper. </i><br /><br />Salacia Deloresista Amanita Trigestatra Zeldana Malifee ... von Humpeding is one of these new recruits. She's a vampire, and a girl, and the traditional racial enemy of werewolves, but Vimes must include her in his team. Sally, as she likes to be known, will ultimately prove to be a valuable member of the City Watch, especially after she makes a guarded pact with her shapechanging Sergeant Angua and with Cheery, the bearded dwarf lady at the Watch reception desk. Their girls' night out in the company of Tawneee, the six foot exotic dancer from the "Girls, Giggles and Garters" night club (the new flame of Corporal Nobby), is as wild as you can expect from this interracial mix:<br /><br /><b>"We've struck a blow for womanhood, Sally declared loudly. Shoes, men, coffins ... never accept the first one you see." </b><br /><br />Similar hidden depths are revealed also by Mr. Pessimal, the mousy auditor nitpicking the receipts of Guard expenses, and by Willikins, the butler at Lady Sybill and Sam's mansion, a useful man to have around if <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ you are at the receiving end of a dwarf terrorist cell attack.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The clues of the case vary from the gruesome and heartbreaking discovery of new bodies in the illicit mining works the dwarfs are digging under Ankh-Morpork to the quirky references to new-age mumbo-jumbo:<br /><br /><i> - Do you believe in the healing power of crystals, young man? snapped the woman, raising the club threateningly. [...] You're not a rock hound yourself, Commander?<br />- I've had the occasional stone thrown at me, said Vimes. I've never bothered to check what kind it was. </i><br /><br />Some of the old charm of early Discworld novels can be still found in this more thoughtful and sharp satire in brief sparkles of irreverent fun:<br /><br /><i> - I'm in deep copro, right? he said.<br />- Certainly we need to talk to you, said Carrot. Do you want a lawyer?<br />- No, I ate already. </i><br /><br />&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;<br /><br /><i> No one read the reports. They appear to be what we in the trade call write-only documents. </i><br /><br />&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;<br /><br />The major tonality of the story remains though one that should give us pause and make us reflect on where do we position ourselves on the issues of the day:<br /><br /><b> What kind of creature defines itself by hatred? </b><br /><br /><b> What kind of human creates his own policeman?<br />One who fears the dark. </b><br /><br />Sam Vimes does his duty not because he gets paid, or because he has sworn some oath or because he is an Ankh-Morpork patriot. He does it because he has to live with himself when he looks in the mirror in the morning while shaving. He does it because he knows there is a darkness inside each of us that must not be allowed to get out and roam free.<br /><br /><i> Beating people up in little rooms ... he knew where that led. And if you did it for a good reason, you'd do it for a bad one. You couldn't say "we're the good guys" and do bad-guy things. Sometimes the watching watch-man inside every good copper's head could use an extra pair of eyes. </i><br /><br />Sam Vimes is an inspiration and a role model to his Watch and to his wards in the city. In Discworld it is still possible to have a chance to turn back the tide of hatred. A dwarf and a troll join Vimes in the search for a solution to the old Koom Valley dillema.<br /><br />Grag (means prophet or shaman in the dwarf culture) Bashfullson rejects the old ways of intransigence: "Some of us move on, sir. Some of us think that darkness isn't a depth, it's a state of mind."<br /><br />Mr Shine, the troll legendary diamond ancestor, says we must start with ourselves if we want change: "And thus we wear down mountains. Water dripping on stone, dissolving and removing. Changing the shape of the world, one drop at a time. Water dripping on a stone, Commander. Water flowing underground, bubbling up in unexpected places."<br /><br />Between them they might redirect the violent streak of their people to a game of <b> Thud </b>, giving a new interpretation to the title of the novel:<br /><br /><i> This is Thud, Mister Vimes, he said, as little stone figures bounced over the board. Dwarfs versus trolls. Eight trolls and thirty-two dwarfs, forever fighting their battles on a cardboard Koom Valley. </i><br /><br />I have tried to leave out the practical details of the investigation and the final outcome, focusing instead on the setting and on the main characters involved. I saved the best for last, the most important thing in life and the deciding factor in Sam Vimes' resolution of the case:<br /><br /><b> This was the book of books, the greatest story ever told. Vimes didn't need to read it anymore. He knew it by heart.<br />It was called "Where's my Cow?"<br />The un-identified complainant has lost their cow. That was the story, really. </b><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7217544700832661865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer8050106204749586901">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads' rel='nofollow noopener'>SpecFic Buddy Reads group</a> in 2020.<br/><br/>The anniversary of the Battle of Koom Valley is approaching, and tensions between the dwarf and troll populations of Ankh-Morpork are escalating quickly. And this leaves Commander Samuel Vimes and the Watch with a difficult job. A job that only gets worse when one of the dwarf community leaders who is stirring up much of the trouble turns up dead deep under the city with a troll club next tot he body.<br/><br/>This is very much a book</span>
  <span id="freeText8050106204749586901" style="display:none">Part of the Pratchett reread with the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/162270-specfic-buddy-reads" rel="nofollow noopener">SpecFic Buddy Reads group</a> in 2020.<br /><br />The anniversary of the Battle of Koom Valley is approaching, and tensions between the dwarf and troll populations of Ankh-Morpork are escalating quickly. And this leaves Commander Samuel Vimes and the Watch with a difficult job. A job that only gets worse when one of the dwarf community leaders who is stirring up much of the trouble turns up dead deep under the city with a troll club next tot he body.<br /><br />This is very much a book of its time, and the last decade or so, as a way to talk about immigrants and cultural values as part of the immigrant experience and particularly when ethnic tensions are rising. It looks at the issues all around, from law enforcement, to community leadership on both sides of increasing and lowering tensions. <br /><br />There's lots of Vimes stuff here as well, including a bit more of Sybil than we've seen for quite a few books. Vimes as a new parent is interesting as well, particularly how being a father has changed his perspective on life.</span>

<div id="review_1358041399" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1358041399" />
    <a title="Richard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8386404-richard"><img alt="Richard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351707681p2/8386404.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1358041399?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard" href="/user/show/8386404-richard">Richard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8386404-richard?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1358041399" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7828200227706166404">8/10<br/><br/>Another sterling effort by Sir Pratchett and a solid entry into the Watch sub-series. I feel like I’m being a bit like a broken record when reviewing these books; they’re all well written, filled with humour (some more than others), great characters and overall a great character development arc (again, some more than others. Has Nobby really changed since the first novel, not really). But the repetition doesn’t impact the overall enjoyment, I’ve found this series filled with great moments th</span>
  <span id="freeText7828200227706166404" style="display:none">8/10<br /><br />Another sterling effort by Sir Pratchett and a solid entry into the Watch sub-series. I feel like I’m being a bit like a broken record when reviewing these books; they’re all well written, filled with humour (some more than others), great characters and overall a great character development arc (again, some more than others. Has Nobby really changed since the first novel, not really). But the repetition doesn’t impact the overall enjoyment, I’ve found this series filled with great moments throughout and only one of the novels hasn’t worked for me and the majority rated at 4*’s.<br /><br />So, rather than talk about the overall plot of the novel which didn’t impact me all that much after seeing similar avenues in the series already explored (i.e. a murder which means further implications between fractious communities) I thought I would mention two stand out moments/things I took from the book which closely relate to each other in a way.<br /><br />The first is a scene where Sam is defending his home from intruders but the fear that his wife and son are in the home at the same time as these intruders and may be in trouble led to some of the most edge of the seat moments I’ve read. Not just in this series, in general. I doff my hat to an author who can put a scene like that in to a “comedy” book and make it work so well.<br /><br />The other is the determination and stubbornness of Vimes spending time at 6pm every night to read to Young Sam, not wanting to let down his son for any reason whatsoever as it would then become something of an easier appointment to break in the future after the first time and later in the book when it becomes harder and harder to keep that promise.<br /><br />This is all from a man who is introduced in the first novel passing out in the street as a drunken alcoholic (can you be a sober alcoholic?). The way his character has developed over the last 7 books has been quite remarkable, quite a lot can be learnt from him and his actions/morals. Hopefully I’ll be half the dad he is when the time comes!! <br /><br />These are fun reads but to call them just that does quite a disservice to them and the author. Pick them up and enjoy them for what they are if you wish, peel back a couple of layers for a funnier look at what’s on offer, peel back even more layers to be truly in awe of a great talent at work with a satirical view on life, politics and the world in general. Only one more in the sub-series to go, quite sad times.<br /><br />If you enjoy this try: “Going Postal” by Terry Pratchett</span>
  <a data-text-id="7828200227706166404" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1871454745" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1871454745" />
    <a title="Esme" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44110461-esme"><img alt="Esme" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1871454745?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Esme" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Esme" href="/user/show/44110461-esme">Esme</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44110461-esme?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44110461-esme?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1871454745" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5983012709807556088">Taking off my Granny Weatherwax hat - this has to be one of the best Discworld novels ever written even though during my first read throughs as a younger person the Watch series didn't resonate with me the way it does now. The more I read Discworld, the more my perspectives on each of the characters changed. <br/><br/>There's a scene in this book that had my heart pounding with the AMAZING trek Sam does through the underworld of the Dwarves. <br/><br/>Holy, shit. If you've read a few discworld books and you're not </span>
  <span id="freeText5983012709807556088" style="display:none">Taking off my Granny Weatherwax hat - this has to be one of the best Discworld novels ever written even though during my first read throughs as a younger person the Watch series didn't resonate with me the way it does now. The more I read Discworld, the more my perspectives on each of the characters changed. <br /><br />There's a scene in this book that had my heart pounding with the AMAZING trek Sam does through the underworld of the Dwarves. <br /><br />Holy, shit. If you've read a few discworld books and you're not impressed, read this book. This is why Brandon Sanderson compared him to Shakespeare. This is why Patrick Rothfuss looked up to him as an inspiring writer. This is why the Queen of England KNIGHTED this man. <br /><br />This book is the culmination of many books which are developing Sam Vimes, from loser drunk alcoholic in a dead end job where the Night Watch is a failing profession. He goes from the lowest of the low to a position of power and leadership - but it's *earned*. You're rooting for him the whole way and when he makes it it's so satisfying. This book encompasses that aspect of his character and it's one of the best payoff arcs of the series. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5983012709807556088" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16442716385848753229">Samuel Vimes, to me, is the most fascinating character in the discworld series - to watch his growth from a disillusioned drunkard in Guards! Guards! up till the current book - fatherhood! I enjoyed it tremendously. I also enjoyed the idea of having the next generation - with young Sam and Tiffany Aching, and trying to imagine little half-werewolf-half-human-brought-up-as-half-dwarf babies... i truly want to get a peek into the future and see what lies in store for the discworld and ankh-morpork</span>
  <span id="freeText16442716385848753229" style="display:none">Samuel Vimes, to me, is the most fascinating character in the discworld series - to watch his growth from a disillusioned drunkard in Guards! Guards! up till the current book - fatherhood! I enjoyed it tremendously. I also enjoyed the idea of having the next generation - with young Sam and Tiffany Aching, and trying to imagine little half-werewolf-half-human-brought-up-as-half-dwarf babies... i truly want to get a peek into the future and see what lies in store for the discworld and ankh-morpork. At the same time, i'm not sure if i'm ready to see the end of my favourite characters quite yet - i'm thinking of the way raymond feist handled the jump into the future.<br /><br />I did have some problems with this book, mainly in terms of pacing and plot devices - but on the whole it was what you'd expect from a typical watch book, with the fascinating addition of a vampire in the watch (!), a character i look forward to seeing in the future books. Didn't quite stand up to Nightwatch, but still had moments - i especially liked the way Vimes reacted to having his family threatened.</span>

  <span id="freeText14001344631501239152" style="display:none">Commander Sam Vimes (do not make the mistake of calling him by his married title of Duke!) is back with is fabulous coppers on the City Watch, along with a both an inspector who has come to see if the larger watch is worth the money, and the first vampire lance-constable. This was the day of a historic battle between trolls and dwarfs--long time enemies. But what is worse is that there are some new dwarfs in town tunneling under the city and bad things are going on down there. On the surface, things aren't so hot, either. <br /><br />I tend to like these books more as I go along in the City Watch series, but also as I have finally learned to read them in a shorter space of time to fit with the pacing of the book. One of my favourite features in this book is the updated imp run reminder device, the Dis-Organizer Mark five <i>Gooseberry</i>™ that is finally useful, but you can't know how without reading the book.</span>

<div id="review_27350898" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27350898" />
    <a title="Melissa McShane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1329059-melissa-mcshane"><img alt="Melissa McShane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1216147749p2/1329059.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27350898?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa McShane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa McShane" href="/user/show/1329059-melissa-mcshane">Melissa McShane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385608671" href="/book/show/833475.Thud_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1329059-melissa-mcshane?shelf=humor">humor</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27350898" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18415446808749591797">Read 6/28/20: I was listening to this, off and on, as an audiobook, taking my time because, let's face it, I've read this book far, far more times than the three Goodreads knows about, and I know it well enough that there wasn't any urgency.<br/><br/>Then the riots happened. And <i>Thud!</i> suddenly became horribly and almost presciently relevant.<br/><br/>This is a book centering on race relations and the police response to them. For a while, I stopped listening to it because it was just too close to current events. Fin</span>
  <span id="freeText18415446808749591797" style="display:none">Read 6/28/20: I was listening to this, off and on, as an audiobook, taking my time because, let's face it, I've read this book far, far more times than the three Goodreads knows about, and I know it well enough that there wasn't any urgency.<br /><br />Then the riots happened. And <i>Thud!</i> suddenly became horribly and almost presciently relevant.<br /><br />This is a book centering on race relations and the police response to them. For a while, I stopped listening to it because it was just too close to current events. Finally, I was able to go back to it, but for the first time in all my readings, it was Sam Vimes's attitude about police work that I was primarily focused on. His constant sense of watching himself because he knows what evil he's capable of, his care not to allow his position--or the position of any of his coppers--to turn dark, struck me as something our real world desperately needs. As with many things Terry Pratchett chose to write about, I think he had an understanding of why a police force is important (and how corrupt it can become without constant vigilance on the part of everyone involved) that is easily overlooked in the general humor that runs through the Discworld novels.<br /><br />I came away from this reading feeling unexpectedly hopeful. Our world is not Discworld, and its problems can't be solved as neatly as a fictional book's can, but the ending of <i>Thud!</i> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[particularly the discovery of how the dwarves and trolls actually made peace at Koom Valley <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> comforts me with the thought that our own solution might not be terribly far away.<br /><br />Read 6/8/18: The Guards novels in the Discworld series are my favorites, and <i>Thud!</i> is maybe my second favorite of the Guards novels. Maybe third. Maybe first. It depends on the day. I love this one enough that I have trouble articulating why it's my second/third/first favorite. The heart of the story, the truth about Koom Valley, strikes me as a powerful narrative about prejudice and hatred and how easy it is to write ourselves a story that fits our preconceived ideas about the world and the people who live in it. But there's also a lot going on around the edges--Sally, the first vampire in the Ankh-Morpork City Watch, and her rivalry with Angua the werewolf, for example. Or Mr. A.E. Pessimal, the auditor, who discovers in himself depths no one could anticipate. And Vimes himself is always a delight. All in all, it's a book I never regret coming back to.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18415446808749591797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6563863-thud">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25923230-youkneek?shelf=completed-series">completed-series</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1973076201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9401492234304752824"><i>Thud!</i> is the seventh book in the <i>Watch</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. Tensions have always been high between the dwarves and the trolls. Now a dwarf has been murdered in Ankh-Morpork, and it looks like a troll might have been responsible. <br/><br/>As with the previous <i>Watch</i> book, it focuses heavily on Vimes, but he continues to be far more likeable than he was earlier in the series so I’m continuing to warm up to him. The story itself didn’t really stand out from the previous books. Yes, it does a good job of p</span>
  <span id="freeText9401492234304752824" style="display:none"><i>Thud!</i> is the seventh book in the <i>Watch</i> subseries of <i>Discworld</i>. Tensions have always been high between the dwarves and the trolls. Now a dwarf has been murdered in Ankh-Morpork, and it looks like a troll might have been responsible. <br /><br />As with the previous <i>Watch</i> book, it focuses heavily on Vimes, but he continues to be far more likeable than he was earlier in the series so I’m continuing to warm up to him. The story itself didn’t really stand out from the previous books. Yes, it does a good job of portraying the conflict and prejudice that can arise between two groups of people. Yes, it has some great things to say through the use of subtle, and not-so-subtle, humor. The problem is, we’ve seen this quite a bit now in <i>Discworld</i>, particularly in the <i>Watch</i> books, so it’s starting to feel a little repetitive. <br /><br />The story did hold my interest, and I wasn’t bored, but I don’t think it will be one of the more memorable stories when I’m looking back on the series. Speaking of which, I’m down to seven books, one illustrated novel, and one short story left to read. I started this series a little under a year ago, on April 26. It will feel weird when I finally finish it and I'm no longer a regular visitor to the Discworld.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9401492234304752824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7642513847612691368">I really really love Douglas Adams, and Thud! book came almost as close to my heart as the Hitchhiker's Guide series. Like Douglas, Pratchett is insanely good at saying the most ridiculous things while sounding totally reasonable and stoically serious. <br/>I especially liked the way Thud! focused on Commander Vimes for the most part but went off on tangents with the characters surrounding him. Tawnee, Angua, Brick, and Sybil were all awesome to hear from and kept the book from slipping into boring. </span>
  <span id="freeText7642513847612691368" style="display:none">I really really love Douglas Adams, and Thud! book came almost as close to my heart as the Hitchhiker's Guide series. Like Douglas, Pratchett is insanely good at saying the most ridiculous things while sounding totally reasonable and stoically serious. <br />I especially liked the way Thud! focused on Commander Vimes for the most part but went off on tangents with the characters surrounding him. Tawnee, Angua, Brick, and Sybil were all awesome to hear from and kept the book from slipping into boring. <br />This is the first Discworld book I've read - I liked it. If I read another one though I'd be curious to hear from the world outside of the Watch and Ankh-Morpork. </span>

<div id="review_1590769545" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1590769545" />
    <a title="Olga Godim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7438487-olga-godim"><img alt="Olga Godim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442639299p2/7438487.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1590769545?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olga Godim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olga Godim" href="/user/show/7438487-olga-godim">Olga Godim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7438487-olga-godim?shelf=fantasy-scifi">fantasy-scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1590769545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2891454370929847110"><b>4.5 stars</b><br/>A terrific novel. It’s part of the City Watch sub-series, and of course, Sam Vimes, the commander of Ankh-Morpork City Watch, is the protagonist. In this novel, he faces a serious crisis. The city streets thrum with unrest. The dwarves and trolls are ready to turn the entire city into a battlefield. The anniversary of the Koom Valley is approaching. <br/>What was Koom Valley? As all of the Discworld know, it is the site of an historic battle between trolls and dwarves. It happened hundreds o</span>
  <span id="freeText2891454370929847110" style="display:none"><b>4.5 stars</b><br />A terrific novel. It’s part of the City Watch sub-series, and of course, Sam Vimes, the commander of Ankh-Morpork City Watch, is the protagonist. In this novel, he faces a serious crisis. The city streets thrum with unrest. The dwarves and trolls are ready to turn the entire city into a battlefield. The anniversary of the Koom Valley is approaching. <br />What was Koom Valley? As all of the Discworld know, it is the site of an historic battle between trolls and dwarves. It happened hundreds of years ago, but the aftereffects still ripple across the Discworld today. The hostilities explode. The tempers fry. And then, a prominent dwarf is murdered. Of course, a troll did it. Didn’t he? <br />While Sam Vimes and his Watch investigate, some self-righteous dwarves obstruct the investigation. It seems, they don’t want the truth to come out. They want history to repeat itself. They want a bloodshed. Besides, the dwarfs are mining under the city, searching for something mystical. Secrets raise their ugly heads. Politics stir. Young enthusiasts bash each other’s craniums. Even Lord Vetinary is concerned by the Watch’s progress. <br />The book isn’t a light satirical romp, as most of the earlier Pratchett’s books are. This one is scary in its intensity. The parallels with the current political situation, with the religious and national conflicts all around the world lead into the gray area of doubts. Sadly, we don’t have our own Sam Vimes to bring around the lasting peace. <br />Still, some scenes of the book are hysterically funny. As the weathered policemen Fred Colon and Nobby discuss art, the reader’s eyes tear from laughter. When they discuss war, it’s not so amusing. When Sam cogitates about a “malign idiot”, a belligerent spiritual leader of the dwarves who incites his compatriots to take up arms, it’s not funny at all. <blockquote>
  <b>Young dwarves listened to him, because he talked about history and destiny and all the other words that always got trotted out to put a gloss on slaughter. It was heady stuff, except that brains were not involved. </b>
</blockquote> Sam doesn’t consider himself a hero. Here he disagrees with the majority of Discworld, as well as with the devoted fans of the series, but his contemplation of police work, while a bit self-derisive, has some truth in it. <blockquote>
  <b>Coppers stayed alive by trickery. That’s how it <i>worked</i>. You had your Watch Houses with the big blue lights outside, and you made certain there were always burly watchmen visible in the big public places, and you swanked around like you own the place. But you didn’t own it. It was all smoke and mirrors. You magicked a little policeman into everyone’s head. You relied on people giving in, knowing the <i>rules</i>. But in truth, a hundred well-armed people could wipe-out the Watch, if they knew what they were doing. </b>
</blockquote> I’m shivery just thinking about it, about all those terrorists and suicide bombers who effectively killed the little watchmen in their heads. I guess I’m not very optimistic, and neither is Sam Vimes, but he is an honest copper and he has my deepest admiration and utmost respect. I salute you, Sam Vimes, and I bow to your creator. <br />A wonderfully powerful book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2891454370929847110" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Chris Gottlieb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1015246-chris-gottlieb"><img alt="Chris Gottlieb" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris Gottlieb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris Gottlieb" href="/user/show/1015246-chris-gottlieb">Chris Gottlieb</a>

          added it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18399923" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9525521773255121943">Sometimes you gotta shut up and pay attention: Sorry, but when you've entertained, amused and jollied up an entire worldful of sapient entities, you *have* to be allowed every now and then to get a bit serious. <br/> <br/>Depends on what makes you laugh, I suppose, but this is not *meant* to be a funny book. Pratchett is doing really serious, relevant and overtly political stuff here. The "funny bits" are no more than the comic relief in the best Shakespearean tradition. <br/> <br/>Parallels are everywhere here. Yo</span>
  <span id="freeText9525521773255121943" style="display:none">Sometimes you gotta shut up and pay attention: Sorry, but when you've entertained, amused and jollied up an entire worldful of sapient entities, you *have* to be allowed every now and then to get a bit serious. <br /> <br />Depends on what makes you laugh, I suppose, but this is not *meant* to be a funny book. Pratchett is doing really serious, relevant and overtly political stuff here. The "funny bits" are no more than the comic relief in the best Shakespearean tradition. <br /> <br />Parallels are everywhere here. You don't need to look for them, they are *obvious*. Considering you're no longer allowed to discuss the real problems of the real world without a screaming mob baying for your blood (you even have to be careful what you name your classroom teddy-bear nowadays, for goodness' sake), you write it in parallel. Like what he's done here. <br /> <br />If you don't like serious, then give this one a miss, but it's one hundred per cent worthy and you'd be missing out on something important.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9525521773255121943" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7748162105786146493">One of the review quotes on the back of this book says something like, "Terry Pratchett's books are almost always better than they need to be." I think this is a pretty good assessment. If Pratchett's books were nothing more than humorous fluff, I would probably still read and enjoy them, but the added depth of character that he brings to his novels is what keeps me reading them over and over again.<br/><br/>In this enstallment, we find Sam Vimes trying to once again bring a semblance of peace to the chao</span>
  <span id="freeText7748162105786146493" style="display:none">One of the review quotes on the back of this book says something like, "Terry Pratchett's books are almost always better than they need to be." I think this is a pretty good assessment. If Pratchett's books were nothing more than humorous fluff, I would probably still read and enjoy them, but the added depth of character that he brings to his novels is what keeps me reading them over and over again.<br /><br />In this enstallment, we find Sam Vimes trying to once again bring a semblance of peace to the chaos that is Anhk-Morpork. The anniversary of Koom Valley, an historic battle between the trolls and the dwarfs, is coming up, and Vimes is doing everything he can to prevent the battle from being re-enacted in the city streets. This isn't easy, especially since a respected dwarf leader has turned up dead, and it looks like a troll may be the culprit. Equally important, Vimes needs to be sure he's home every night to read to Young Sam from The Book, aka <i>Where is My Cow?</i>.<br /></span>
