        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer460623708" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12264351792323169807">4.5 stars. Great addition to the Discworld "City Watch" sub-series (books listed in order below). THUD features Commander Sam Vimes, Corporal Carrot, and the whole City Watch -- humans, trolls, werewolves, dwarfs, etc. <br/><br/>Happy to say, the story includes several vivid and heartwarming scenes with Sam's wife Sybil, their one-year-old son Sam, and their resourceful butler, Willikins. Throughout this book there's a theme of promise-keeping. Daddy (Commander Sam Vimes) never breaks his word to his chil</span>
  <span id="freeText12264351792323169807" style="display:none">4.5 stars. Great addition to the Discworld "City Watch" sub-series (books listed in order below). THUD features Commander Sam Vimes, Corporal Carrot, and the whole City Watch -- humans, trolls, werewolves, dwarfs, etc. <br><br>Happy to say, the story includes several vivid and heartwarming scenes with Sam's wife Sybil, their one-year-old son Sam, and their resourceful butler, Willikins. Throughout this book there's a theme of promise-keeping. Daddy (Commander Sam Vimes) never breaks his word to his child (one mustn't), and he promised Sam that he would always be home by 6:00 pm to read a bed-time story to him. Sam's favorite storybook is <i><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34524.Where_s_My_Cow___Discworld___34_5_" title="Where's My Cow? (Discworld, #34.5) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Where's My Cow?</a>, </i> which — in the way of children — must be read over and over and over again, until it becomes a treasured childhood tradition.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1438381789i/15700787.jpg" width="380" height="320" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A new member joins the Watch, at Lord Vetinari's insistence, and against the gut instincts of Vimes and Angua. Sally is a gorgeous young-looking vampire who doesn't drink human blood. She's a member of the temperance league, a black-ribboner. A good chunk of the story dwells on Angua's insecurity around the svelte, sophisticated vampire (too much focus on that). <br><br>We also meet the mysterious Mr. Shine and Inspector A.E. Pessimal, as well as Nobby's girlfriend, Tawneee (too much about her).<br><br>Of course, it wouldn't be Discworld without another murder mystery to solve and a wild adventure (this time with warring trolls and dwarfs in the city and far away in Koom Valley) and enough pithy puns and cynical satire to sink a ship. (Actually, not as many witticisms as seen in prior books, but still enough for me.)<br><br>Plot: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br>Some dwarves are digging under Ankh-Morpork, looking for an ancient cube. Some of these down-deep diggers are murdered in the city, and the priestly fundamentalist hooded dwarves (called grags) are blaming the trolls. The eerie sign of The Summoning Dark keeps appearing at murder sites. Intense racism, superstition, guilt and hostility runs high, and battle lines are forming up, right in the middle of Ankh-Morpork. Hostilities escalate right into Ramkin House on Scoone Avenue, where little Sam sleeps. This brings out the leashed beast in Commander Vimes (Good scene).<br><br>Storytelling time is precisely 6:00. Not 6:15. Not 6:30. But what happens when it looks like Daddy just can't make it home from work on time? The whole City Watch makes damn sure he can, that's what. <br><br>But what about when Daddy is trapped in the belly of the world, facing the Summoning Dark in the Valley of the Shadow of Death, aka Koom Valley? This is sacred land, where the battle between the trolls and dwarfs took place in ages past, with King of the Low Dwarfs, B'hrian Bloodaxe.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br><br>In the end, it all comes down to pure cussedness. Determination. Far from home, fighting for his life, surrounded by smothering, summoning darkness, will Daddy find a way to read WHERE'S MY COW to young Sam on time?? <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1437857557i/15637070.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Quibbles: </b> Some of the relationships within the City Watch disappointed me a little. Some infighting, jealousy, etc. Sometimes, the tone gets a wee bit teachy / preachy, and some puns have me rolling my eyes, but still, jolly good stuff, complete with political satire and family values. <br><br>Plot contrivance: I couldn't swallow that it took days for Vimes to hear the big news that Methodia Rascal's famous 50-foot painting of the Battle of Koom Valley was stolen. (It happened days ago and would have been in the newspaper and the Watch House gossip.) But this allowed the cool scene when Vimes thumped the table in anger, scattering board game and making candles fall over.<br><br>See my review of the City Watch series embedded in my review of Guards! Guards!<br><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/460623616">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br><br><b> Series:</b> Here are the Discworld books about the City Watch, in order:<br><br>Guards! Guards! (1989) <br>Theatre of Cruelty (4 pages, free, online)<br>Men at Arms (1993)<br>Feet of Clay (1996)<br>Jingo (1997) <br>The Fifth Elephant (1999) <br>Night Watch (2002) <br>Thud (2005) <br>Snuff (2011) <br>(Series links at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/106221-discworld---ankh-morpork-city-watch">https://www.goodreads.com/series/1062...</a>)<br><br>Oh--and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6250169.Unseen_Academicals__Discworld___37__Rincewind__8_" title="Unseen Academicals (Discworld, #37; Rincewind #8) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Unseen Academicals</a> (2009) is not really City Watch, but a few characters overlap.<br><br><b>Related "Modernization" Series: </b> Also, there are some spin-off books that cross-over with the "City Watch" sub-series. These books introduce new-fangled ideas to the twin cities of Ankh-Morpork. The books feature <b>con-man turned hero, Moist Von Lipvig, </b> and a few characters from the City Watch. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64222.Going_Postal__Discworld___33__Moist_von_Lipwig___1_" title="Going Postal (Discworld, #33; Moist von Lipwig, #1) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Going Postal</a> describes the invention and proliferation of postage stamps (loved it) and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/116296.Making_Money__Discworld___36_" title="Making Money (Discworld, #36) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Making Money</a>, depicts the creation of paper money to replace gold (also very good). Then there is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11275323.Raising_Steam__Discworld___40_" title="Raising Steam (Discworld, #40) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Raising Steam</a>, where Lord Vetinari makes Moist Von Lipvig introduce the steam train to the City, making it popular and profitable (not sure Pratchett wrote it -- not his best).<br><br>Also, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567670.The_Truth__Discworld___25_" title="The Truth (Discworld, #25) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">The Truth</a> depicts the invention of a printing press and the subsequent introduction of widespread newspapers into society. Satire deals with the wholesale acceptance of lies when ink-validated. William de Worde (he-he) is the city's first investigative journalist, sleuthing out possible murder and a plot to overthrow Lord Vetinari. This book does not feature Moist Von Lipvig, but it has some City Watch characters. <br><br>Finally, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34510.Moving_Pictures__Discworld___10_" title="Moving Pictures (Discworld, #10) by Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Moving Pictures</a> introduces the film industry to the eager citizens of Ankh-Morpork. This book does not include Moist Von Lipvig. I didn't care as much for it, but it's brimming with satire and some scenes are great.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="12264351792323169807" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer14062571445512945065">I believe that this is my favorite Discworld novel that I've read so far! (And I've read quite a few of them.)<br/>This one transcends the one-note satire that many of the Discworld books feature (each one takes on a certain topic - this one has a theme of racial tolerance). This installment of the series also pokes gentle fun at the mystery genre - but, while doing so, is itself a quite good mystery novel!<br/>It actually kicks the crap out of any of the glut of 'paranormal investigation' novels that hav</span>
  <span id="freeText14062571445512945065" style="display:none">I believe that this is my favorite Discworld novel that I've read so far! (And I've read quite a few of them.)<br />This one transcends the one-note satire that many of the Discworld books feature (each one takes on a certain topic - this one has a theme of racial tolerance). This installment of the series also pokes gentle fun at the mystery genre - but, while doing so, is itself a quite good mystery novel!<br />It actually kicks the crap out of any of the glut of 'paranormal investigation' novels that have become quite the trend of late... by any measure. <br /><br />The premise - a dwarf turns up dead. The club lying next to his body would seem to indicate that a troll did the job. But wait - that clue is so obvious that it seems it must've been planted. Perhaps it's a plot to discredit trolls? But then - a troll WAS actually at the scene of the crime!<br /><br />Commander Vimes of the City Watch must investigate - but the particulars of this case aren't the only thing he has to worry about, as he tries to manage the potential conflicts between his employees - especially the werewolf Sergeant Angua and his new vampire recruit - and the city seems to be sliding toward full-blown ethnic conflict. <br /></span>

<div id="review_1282743261" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1282743261" />
    <a title="Angela Blount" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4269099-angela-blount"><img alt="Angela Blount" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592507072p2/4269099.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1282743261?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela Blount" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela Blount" href="/user/show/4269099-angela-blount">Angela Blount</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4269099-angela-blount?shelf=goddaughter-approved-picks">goddaughter-approved-picks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4269099-angela-blount?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1282743261" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1918522240366361581"><br/><b> <i>“Shoes, men, coffins; never accept the first one you see.”</i></b>  <br/><br/>This is my first true experience with Terry Pratchet, and I’m left with the impression that he is (and was) to the fantasy genre what Douglas Adams was to sci-fi. That is to say… expansively clever, decidedly British, and not prone to taking himself too seriously. The man was a brilliant writer and masterful storyteller—of that I’m convinced. The following review will reflect this particular Discworld book only…<br/><br/>Samuel Vimes, the no-non</span>
  <span id="freeText1918522240366361581" style="display:none"><br><b> <i>“Shoes, men, coffins; never accept the first one you see.”</i></b>  <br><br>This is my first true experience with Terry Pratchet, and I’m left with the impression that he is (and was) to the fantasy genre what Douglas Adams was to sci-fi. That is to say… expansively clever, decidedly British, and not prone to taking himself too seriously. The man was a brilliant writer and masterful storyteller—of that I’m convinced. The following review will reflect this particular Discworld book only…<br><br>Samuel Vimes, the no-nonsense Commander of the Ankh-Morpork City Watch, has his work cut out for him. With the upcoming anniversary of Koom Valley—a historic battle between trolls and dwarfs—all signs point to escalating hostility between the two species within his otherwise well-integrated city. Someone, or <i>something</i>, is out to restart an old war. When a dwarf is murdered and a troll blamed, it’s up to Vimes and his loyal, diverse police force to somehow keep the peace… and perhaps prevent another Koom Valley in their midst. Time for Sam to break out the hard-boiled investigative skills.<br><br><i> <b>“Why bother with a cunning plan when a simple one will do?” </b> </i> <br><br>From early on my brain decided the character of Sam Vimes should be cast by David Tenant—probably because his Broadchurch persona seemed suitably applicable (with a dash of Dr. Who thrown in for my own amusement.) But it wasn’t until we meet Sam’s toddler son, and learn that Sam has made it an unshakeable paternal priority to read to said son at precisely 6PM every evening, that I truly became attached to the character. And in this reader’s opinion, the threading through of the children’s book played a large role in the keen and endearing genius of this whole tale.<br> <br><i>“WHERE’S MY COW? ARE YOU MY COW?” </i><br><br>It’s safe to conclude that Pratchett isn’t just parodying the fantasy genre (although, he’s delightfully good at it!), he’s parodying—and in his own way, confronting—a complex menagerie of issues. Western society, racial tensions, outmoded traditionalism, historical revisionism, cultural chauvinism, drug use… he takes aim at all of these and gets away with it thanks to an expert layering of guile, tongue-in-cheek humor, and measured poignancy. In this reader’s mind, that makes him an intrepid social commentator. <br> <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1436814508i/15506562._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>(Considering the creatures inhabiting Discworld are from an array of TRULY different races (i.e. species), it roundly highlights the absurdity of this reality’s ethnic and cultural divides.) <br><br>The narrative jumps around a bit, including a range of third person perspectives meant to give readers insight into the larger political and social situation. The characterization is whimsical and precise, the dialogue is pithy, and the world-building is deftly handled. Everything weaves around and back together with a thoroughness that feels like a gift I didn’t even realize I wanted until Pratchett handed it to me, all tied up neat and pretty. <br><br>If I had one complaint, it would be the length. There were times the action was low for extended periods and it felt almost as though the author was rabbit-trailing. (Oh me of little faith, I know.) Of course, some of this could simply be a result of perfectly necessary scenes involving characters that I had less investment in. In any case, my interest sometimes wandered more than I, personally, would have liked. <br><br>Favorite quote:<br><br><b> <i>“His mind worked fast, flying in emergency supplies of common sense, as human minds do, to construct a huge anchor in sanity and prove that what happened hadn't really happened and, if it had happened, hadn't happened much.”</i></b> <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="1918522240366361581" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_250340004" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/250340004" />
    <a title="Fiona" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7232207-fiona"><img alt="Fiona" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472071438p2/7232207.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/250340004?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fiona" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fiona" href="/user/show/7232207-fiona">Fiona</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7232207-fiona?shelf=gentleman-detectives">gentleman-detectives</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7232207-fiona?shelf=warning-magic-ahead">warning-magic-ahead</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7232207-fiona?shelf=politics-exclamation-mark">politics-exclamation-mark</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer250340004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15645861116114063381">Oh, he's good. He's very good. Sam Vimes and the City Watch is the only Discworld arc that I don't think I've ever tired of two thirds of the way through a book, and I'm so glad I picked this one up. It's a very well-crafted whodunnit, with a rich and complicated backdrop and a well-rounded and thoroughly interesting cast. The pace is spot on. The parallels to the real world, and the Serious Issues, while undeniably serious and difficult, are not particularly in-your-face or preachey. Well, may </span>
  <span id="freeText15645861116114063381" style="display:none">Oh, he's good. He's very good. Sam Vimes and the City Watch is the only Discworld arc that I don't think I've ever tired of two thirds of the way through a book, and I'm so glad I picked this one up. It's a very well-crafted whodunnit, with a rich and complicated backdrop and a well-rounded and thoroughly interesting cast. The pace is spot on. The parallels to the real world, and the Serious Issues, while undeniably serious and difficult, are not particularly in-your-face or preachey. Well, may be a tiny little bit, towards the end. But you can't be the world's straightest copper and not get a little bit preachey, and Pratchett did spend the vast majority of the book impressively managing to avoid it.<br /><br />I have also, on a side note, discovered that "Where's My Cow?" is an actual real-life book (a Discworld companion one, of course). I don't know why this should amuse me so much, but I'm quite tempted to try and track it down.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15645861116114063381" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2601139659" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2601139659" />
    <a title="Ric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35905360-ric"><img alt="Ric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583635231p2/35905360.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2601139659?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ric" href="/user/show/35905360-ric">Ric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060815318" href="/book/show/386375.Thud_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35905360-ric?shelf=2019-reading-challenge">2019-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35905360-ric?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2601139659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11853481954636084771"><i>“War, Nobby. Huh! What is it good for?” <br/>“Dunno, Sarge. Freeing slaves, maybe?” <br/>“Absol-well okay.” <br/>“Defending yourself against a totalitarian aggressor?” <br/>“All right, I’ll grant you that, but-“ <br/>“Saving civilization from a horde of-“ <br/>“It doesn’t do any good in the long run is what I’m saying Nobby, if you’d listen for five seconds together.”</i><br/><br/>Yes, that is real text from this book, and if you’ve ever read anything Sir Terry has written that isn’t really surprising. His writing style contains fantastic</span>
  <span id="freeText11853481954636084771" style="display:none"><i>“War, Nobby. Huh! What is it good for?” <br />“Dunno, Sarge. Freeing slaves, maybe?” <br />“Absol-well okay.” <br />“Defending yourself against a totalitarian aggressor?” <br />“All right, I’ll grant you that, but-“ <br />“Saving civilization from a horde of-“ <br />“It doesn’t do any good in the long run is what I’m saying Nobby, if you’d listen for five seconds together.”</i><br /><br />Yes, that is real text from this book, and if you’ve ever read anything Sir Terry has written that isn’t really surprising. His writing style contains fantastic humor weaved into these great stories, and it makes Discworld a truly special series. But Thud! might be the one who’s theme holds up most today, and it isn’t necessarily a good one. <br /><br />It’s about the trolls and dwarves long-standing hatred of one another and constant warfare of the two surrounding one holiday on the Disc. If that isn’t a little on the nose when it comes to hatred among certain groups in our current society, I don’t know how much else I can add to that. But that quote (even though it’s a play on Edwin Starr’s “War” in the best possible way, actually kinda says a lot, mostly in that last line. The book is a great commentary on racism, hatred, and extremism that still (unfortunately) holds up today. <br /><br />However, it’s not all dark, because this book was incredibly entertaining. The Watch has never been my favorite because Vimes was never my favorite, I always thought side characters like Carrot and Angua always made this sub-series shine. And they did in this one, but I loved Vimes’ role in all of this as well. There was also a very meta cameo from Death, which might have been my favorite Death appearance outside of his series. Thud! was one of the top 10 Discworld novels I've come across.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11853481954636084771" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3110913153" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3110913153" />
    <a title="Trish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3461034-trish"><img alt="Trish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588663568p2/3461034.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3110913153?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trish" href="/user/show/3461034-trish">Trish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781407032658" href="/book/show/45154608-thud">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3110913153" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18104814211939518895">6 o'clock in the evening. It's when I started this review and, coincidentally, a very significant time every day in the life of Sam Vimes - both senior and junior. That's right, Commander Vimes is "dada" to a 14-months-old boy now. But never fear, that doesn't stop him from investigating a murder in Ankh-Morpork that could mean war between dwarves and trolls and which thus might rip apart the entire city. In between he also handles employment issues and still fights with his disorganizer (newer </span>
  <span id="freeText18104814211939518895" style="display:none">6 o'clock in the evening. It's when I started this review and, coincidentally, a very significant time every day in the life of Sam Vimes - both senior and junior. That's right, Commander Vimes is "dada" to a 14-months-old boy now. But never fear, that doesn't stop him from investigating a murder in Ankh-Morpork that could mean war between dwarves and trolls and which thus might rip apart the entire city. In between he also handles employment issues and still fights with his disorganizer (newer model now) and is very much in love with his incredible wife.<br>Why is (t)his family situation important? Because it comes under threat by various parties but guess what: the Watch has his back and it was making me tear up.<br>Back to the crime at the heart of this story though. Dwarves and trolls have been enemies a long time ago. Mind you, both dwarves and trolls think that the ones living in Ankh-Morpork aren't ACTUAL dwarves and trolls - too many compromises, modern living situations, weird clothing, allowing females to identify as females ...! The Watch consists of all kinds of citizens and they get along more or less - until a dwarf is murdered and a troll club is found at the scene. Supposedly. Then, a radical party is setting up camp and literally undermines the city so you can imagine the kind of trouble the Patrician and the Watch find themselves in.<br>While there is a limit to what Vimes can stomach, he finds out again and again that he doesn't actually have limits - because he cares, because he's a good copper, a good boss, a good husband, a good father ... even if he doesn't always know it himself and even if „higher powers“ are trying to manipulate him for their own designs.<br><br>In case it wasn't clear yet, I'm a Vimes-fangirl. But, equally, I love the Watch. At least the core members. They might fight sometimes, but when the shit hits the fan, they stand together and fight for each other. Thus, the newest addition was a delight. On top of Detritus the troll, Angua the werewolf, Carrot the human dwarf (yes, it's a thing), Cheery the actual but female dwarf ... we now have <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Sally the vampire and she fit right in (forget that little hiccup). Especially that girls’ night out … <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> BWAHAHAHAHAHAHAHA! And can we just take a moment to celebrate Willikins?!<br><br>All <i>Discworld</i> novels are wonderful. Some are fantastic on top of that. And then there are the ones where you are desperately trying to explain to people why they mean so much to you. But no amount of quotes or thrusting the book under peoples’ noses will ever come close to doing the brilliance justice.<br>That is the problem I'm facing with this one (amongst others). Yes, it has partly to do with the reader knowing these characters so intimately by now (which is one of the strongest reasons why I’m recommending a chronological approach to reading this series). <br>The other part is that Pratchett had such a brilliant mind - he saw tiny little things, easy to overlook but he would notice them. When writing these books, he would then remember and twist them, bend them into a knot and use them as embellishments for the tapestry of weirdness that is this series.<br>But it's more than that. There are big issues here as well (as usual). Like racism (even within a community). Like doing something without actually believing it yourself just because it has always been that way, not questioning what you're told, not trying to fight for a better world just because continuing down this path is easier. Or like threats to one's family (whether we're talking blood-relations or the families we make). And many other things. <br>All that heaviness is presented with Pratchett's signature humour, which is silly at some times and very dark at others - just the way I like it and always in the perfect combination.<br><br>After 34 volumes in this series, I’m bound to repeat myself. It’s all true though. And no, you never get enough of it.<br><br>By the way, the actual game <i>Thud!</i> exists. And yes, of course I have it! ;)<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1585727319i/29210094._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>(And yes, <i>Where's My Cow?</i> is also an actual book you can buy.)</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="18104814211939518895" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41209964?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lightreads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lightreads" href="/user/show/1836077-lightreads">Lightreads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1836077-lightreads?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41209964" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1428011824045445719">Most recent Watch novel. My very favorite Discworld arc, so I've doled them out carefully to myself over the past few years. This one lives up and then some. Sam Vimes and his men coppers face civil unrest as racial tensions flare between the dwarves and the trolls. Meanwhile a mysterious museum theft may have surprising consequences, there's been a murder, and Vimes must get home by six to read "Where's My Cow?" to his son.<br/><br/>It's a Watch novel – either you know why it's awesome and you're already</span>
  <span id="freeText1428011824045445719" style="display:none">Most recent Watch novel. My very favorite Discworld arc, so I've doled them out carefully to myself over the past few years. This one lives up and then some. Sam Vimes and his men coppers face civil unrest as racial tensions flare between the dwarves and the trolls. Meanwhile a mysterious museum theft may have surprising consequences, there's been a murder, and Vimes must get home by six to read "Where's My Cow?" to his son.<br /><br />It's a Watch novel – either you know why it's awesome and you're already smiling, or you don't and you should stop listening to me and go off and find out. Except listen long enough to know these books are hilarious, and also the sort of sneaky wonderful that makes you sit up in the middle of the page and snortgiggle up the tear you just cried without actually realizing it. Particular highlights for me in this one were the changes in Sam upon the birth of his son and the consequent exploration of Sam's untapped potential for violence in the name of right. Also, I laughed a lot. Also also, Vetinari. <br /><br />Who knows what's to come, but if this is the last Watch novel we get, the world will be just a little bit less bright.</span>

<div id="review_196192147" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/196192147" />
    <a title="Wastrel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5743748-wastrel"><img alt="Wastrel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317660831p2/5743748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/196192147?book_show_action=true">Nov 18, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wastrel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wastrel" href="/user/show/5743748-wastrel">Wastrel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385608671" href="/book/show/833475.Thud_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=fantastika-social-fantasy">fantastika-social-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=r-un">r-un</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=z-2005">z-2005</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer196192147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4726527039323096465">How pleasing is <i>Thud!</i>? Well, it depend what you're looking for.<br/>If you're looking for the next chapter in the story of our beloved characters in the Watch, it's pretty disappointing. Far too much of what we are given here feels like repetition from earlier novels, and much of the rest is ultimately trivial and goes nowhere. Neither the characters nor the themes here are substantially advanced from previous installments, and as a result I was deeply disappointed when I first read this. I had been </span>
  <span id="freeText4726527039323096465" style="display:none">How pleasing is <i>Thud!</i>? Well, it depend what you're looking for.<br />If you're looking for the next chapter in the story of our beloved characters in the Watch, it's pretty disappointing. Far too much of what we are given here feels like repetition from earlier novels, and much of the rest is ultimately trivial and goes nowhere. Neither the characters nor the themes here are substantially advanced from previous installments, and as a result I was deeply disappointed when I first read this. I had been waiting so many years for a new Watch novel, after the brilliant double punch of <i>The Fifth Elephant</i> and <i>Night Watch</i> and THIS is what I was given!? And it didn't help that the novel itself is horribly anticlimactic, with an ending that I don't think works on any level, and that the whole thing feels a little bloated compared to the earlier books, while simultaneously feeling rushed.<br /><br />But now I read it differently. Terry Pratchett is dead, there will be no more Discworld, and the disconsolate reader must find their fix where they can get it. So if you're just looking for another little fix of Pratchett... well then suddenly the book is a lot more impressive. It's pretty clever. It's really tense. It's amazingly funny - it's not the funniest thing he's written, by some margin, but I'd sort of expected that by this point he'd never be this funny again, that wry smiles were all we were to expect. Instead, I laughed out loud on multiple occasions. <br /><br />Look at it this way: imagine Discworld is your favourite network TV show. <i>Thud!</i> is one of those episodes you get really frustrated by when it turns up one week: it's a bland procedural episode that you've sort of seen before; it doesn't really advance the overarching plot of the season; it has the characters acting stupidly and annoyingly - not out of character exactly, but the from the annoying sides of their character; there's no great structural invention, no unusual theme, no stand-out scene or performance, no famous lines you'll be quoting for years; it's not even memorably awful, it's just... one of those forgettable episodes. And when you've waited all week for it, you may even get a bit pissed off that they're wasting your time with this stuff.<br /><br />And yet, when the show's over, and you miss it, and you turn on the TV and happen to catch an episode and it's this one, suddenly... well, you see it differently. Because it's not an unusual episode, an outstanding one, a landmark one, it's just one of the normal, representative ones. It does what that show did, and it does it really pretty well, and it's exciting to watch and it's funny and you get to spend time with the characters, and maybe you even come away glad that that's the episode you caught, rather than one of the big dramatic ones that's maybe better quality but you don't quite get the time to just relax in the company of your TV-show-'friends' and watch them at work on an ordinary day. <i>Thud!</i> is that kind of book. <br /><br />(For further explanation of this view, you can <a href="https://vacuouswastrel.wordpress.com/2016/11/11/thud-by-terry-pratchett/" rel="nofollow noopener">see my longer review on my blog</a>)</span>
  <a data-text-id="4726527039323096465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <td colspan="2">

<span id="freeTextContainer2303711975627009350">Trolls and dwarves don't get along. at least so tradition holds. The big city tends to blur these ethnic-cultural traditions, but some will always make it their business to keep stirring the plot...<br/> In the sprawling Discworld series, the Watch books are a subplot of hardboiled crime fiction, through the eyes (and noses etc) of Ankh-Morpork's City Watch. Thud! throws us into the thick of it, with big-city intercultural tensions, deep darkness, politics and policing, as well as boundless in-jokes,</span>
  <span id="freeText2303711975627009350" style="display:none">Trolls and dwarves don't get along. at least so tradition holds. The big city tends to blur these ethnic-cultural traditions, but some will always make it their business to keep stirring the plot...<br /> In the sprawling Discworld series, the Watch books are a subplot of hardboiled crime fiction, through the eyes (and noses etc) of Ankh-Morpork's City Watch. Thud! throws us into the thick of it, with big-city intercultural tensions, deep darkness, politics and policing, as well as boundless in-jokes, and the ocassional cameo from Death - so don your riot gear. This is prime Pratchett!</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2583044064" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16037241747123284271">I'm realizing yet another flaw in this book rating system. So, for instance, this is not my favorite of Pratchett's books, although I enjoyed it a lot, BUT, this is Terry Pratchett. Terry Pratchett. Now Terry Pratchett in my estimation is a 5* writer at the worst of times and deserves credit for it. So I wish I had a two-tiered rating system or something. I would give Terry Pratchett 5*, and this particular book 3*, if you see what I'm getting at.<br/>All that aside, after reading this, I am more con</span>
  <span id="freeText16037241747123284271" style="display:none">I'm realizing yet another flaw in this book rating system. So, for instance, this is not my favorite of Pratchett's books, although I enjoyed it a lot, BUT, this is Terry Pratchett. Terry Pratchett. Now Terry Pratchett in my estimation is a 5* writer at the worst of times and deserves credit for it. So I wish I had a two-tiered rating system or something. I would give Terry Pratchett 5*, and this particular book 3*, if you see what I'm getting at.<br />All that aside, after reading this, I am more convinced than ever that leadership training should include the reading of all the City Watch series. Kings used to have Fools to make fun of them and help them laugh at themselves - I think Pratchett is essential in that same way. Quite a lot of what we do is utterly ridiculous. There's no one like Terry for pointing it out.<br /></span>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('lqaD/yKuiVG1gOP2geJNFh342NwyKkInXYBMuULzhRWsgOotmfBAORKrV5d2F0fJy6c/FPynzEAG/goIj7B3jw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mJ0l6Ni5V7FJupOBzWgYHpVcI2kMLGOWPyLE/pDmlSqiu0w6Y+ee2e6RJ+A6nRLBQwPEocKh7fFkXIJPXaVnsA==')})
  }
//]]>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett">About Terry Pratchett</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1235562205p3/1654.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14957108968120442754">Born Terence David John Pratchett, Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <i>The Carpet People</i>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br/><br/>Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, i</span>
  <span id="freeText14957108968120442754" style="display:none">Born Terence David John Pratchett, Sir Terry Pratchett sold his first story when he was thirteen, which earned him enough money to buy a second-hand typewriter. His first novel, a humorous fantasy entitled <i>The Carpet People</i>, appeared in 1971 from the publisher Colin Smythe. <br /><br />Terry worked for many years as a journalist and press officer, writing in his spare time and publishing a number of novels, including his first Discworld novel, <i>The Color of Magic</i>, in 1983. In 1987, he turned to writing full time. <br /><br />There are over 40 books in the Discworld series, of which four are written for children. The first of these, <i>The Amazing Maurice and His Educated Rodents</i>, won the Carnegie Medal. <br /><br />A non-Discworld book, <i>Good Omens</i>, his 1990 collaboration with Neil Gaiman, has been a longtime bestseller and was reissued in hardcover by William Morrow in early 2006 (it is also available as a mass market paperback - Harper Torch, 2006 - and trade paperback - Harper Paperbacks, 2006). <br /><br />In 2008, Harper Children's published Terry's standalone non-Discworld YA novel, <i>Nation</i>. Terry published <i>Snuff</i> in October 2011. <br /><br />Regarded as one of the most significant contemporary English-language satirists, Pratchett has won numerous literary awards, was named an Officer of the British Empire (OBE) “for services to literature” in 1998, and has received honorary doctorates from the University of Warwick in 1999, the University of Portsmouth in 2001, the University of Bath in 2003, the University of Bristol in 2004, Buckinghamshire New University in 2008, the University of Dublin in 2008, Bradford University in 2009, the University of Winchester in 2009, and The Open University in 2013 for his contribution to Public Service.<br /><br />In Dec. of 2007, Pratchett disclosed that he had been diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease. On 18 Feb, 2009, he was knighted by Queen Elizabeth II.<br /><br />He was awarded the World Fantasy Life Achievement Award in 2010.<br /><br />Sir Terry Pratchett passed away on 12th March 2015.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14957108968120442754" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('bookCover_34510').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('bookCover_34510').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('bookCover_34510').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>
</div>
